Hydrogen bonds, which occur between a hydrogen donor (such as -OH or -NH groups) and an acceptor (like oxygen or nitrogen atoms), provide directional and relatively strong interactions that help define the specificity and orientation of guest molecules within a host cavity. These interactions contribute to the selectivity and binding strength of the complex, often dictating the overall architecture of the assembly.\n\nAnion-π interactions involve the attraction between an anionic species and the π-electron system of aromatic rings, which can be electron-deficient. These interactions are particularly important in systems where the guest contains negatively charged groups or in the stabilization of anionic guests within a host framework. They can influence the binding affinity and selectivity, especially in cases where hydrogen bonding alone is insufficient.\n\nTogether, these non-covalent interactions enable the reversible and selective recognition of guest molecules by hosts, facilitating the formation of stable yet dynamic supramolecular assemblies. The interplay of hydrogen bonds and anion-π interactions allows for fine-tuning of binding properties, which is crucial in applications like molecular sensors, drug delivery, and molecular machines. Understanding these interactions at a detailed level involves considering the electronic properties of the host and guest, the geometry of binding sites, and the solvent environment, all of which are Hydrogen bonds, which occur between a hydrogen donor (such as -OH or -NH groups) and an acceptor (like oxygen or nitrogen atoms), provide directional and relatively strong interactions that help define the specificity and orientation of guest molecules within a host cavity. These interactions contribute to the selectivity and binding strength of the complex, often dictating the overall architecture of the assembly.\n\nAnion-π interactions involve the attraction between an anionic species and the π-electron system of aromatic rings, which can be electron-deficient. These interactions are particularly important in systems where the guest contains negatively charged groups or in the stabilization of anionic guests within a host framework. They can influence the binding affinity and selectivity, especially in cases where hydrogen bonding alone is insufficient.\n\nTogether, these non-covalent interactions enable the reversible and selective recognition of guest molecules by hosts, facilitating the formation of stable yet dynamic supramolecular assemblies. The interplay of hydrogen bonds and anion-π interactions allows for fine-tuning of binding properties, which is crucial in applications like molecular sensors, drug delivery, and molecular machines. Understanding these interactions at a detailed level involves considering the electronic properties of the host and guest, the geometry of binding sites, and the solvent environment, all of which are
